Justice of the Peace Has
\ Power to Admin-*
ister Oath!
â ' â¢
Special 'Dispatch to The Call.
EW YORK, Sept. 13.â Vice Pres
ident Roosevelt will probably be
sworn in without delay to fill
| the office made vacant, by the
> death of McKinley. It Is not
necessary even that a Supreme
Court Judge should administer
the oath. This may be done by a Justice
of the Peace.
Theodore Roosevelt is the fifth Vice
President who has become President in
this way. The first was John Tyler, who
succeeded William Henry Harrison in
1841. The next was Millard Fillmore, who
became President after the death of
Zachary Taylor In 1850; Andrew Johnson
became President on the death of Abra
ham Lincoln in 1S65, "and when James' A.
Garfield died in 18S1, Chester A. Arthur
became President.
It is twenty years ago with the excep
tion of seven days since Vice President
Arthur assumed the highest office in the
land. In less than four hours after death
Â¦ had claimed President Garfield, the vic
tim of Guiteau's bullet, General Arthur
had been sworn In as chief executive of
the United States.
Death of Garfield.
It was at twenty-five minutes to 11
o'clock on the night of September 19, 1881,
that President Garfleld breathed his last
in a cottage in Elberon, N. J. At once a
telegram bearing the sad news was dis
patched by Attorney General MacVeagh
to Vice President Arthur, who was in his
home on Lexington avenue, this city.
With General Arthur were District Attor
ney Rollins, Police Commissioner French
and Kliliu Root, who is now Secretary of
. War.
v After consultation with these gentle
man it was decided by the Vice President
that he should take the oath of office
- without delay. Leaving him with Â¦ his
' private secretary. Colonel J. C. Reed, the
three gentlemen named left the house
about 1 o'clock In the morning. When
Mr. Root and Mr. Rollins returned in a
carriage they had with them Judge R.
Brady of the Supreme Court. - Scarcely
liad they entered the house when Mr.
French drove up and with him was Judge
Donohu<*, also of the Supreme Court. It
Jiad been deemed the wisest course -to
send to the homes of two judges so that
if either was away the services of the
other could be available.
Scene in Arthur's House.
The party assembled in ti.e front part
of General Arthur's house, a big room
pHelved with books, with rare old paint
ings on the walls and a bust of Henry
Clay in one corner. General Arthur stood
at a. table in the center of the room with
Â¦ the others clustered about him. As Judge
Brady had been the first to arrive, to him
fell the honor of the oath
of fealty to the Government. It was a
simple ceremony, but it was impressive,
and all of those present felt the solemnity
1 . of the occasion. From a book Judge
* Brady read the oath:
â¢ "I do solemnly swear that I will faith
j fully execute the office of President of the
! United States and will, to the best of my
ability, preserve, protect and defend the
constitution of the United States."
So deeply was General Arthur affected
as he endeavored to repeat these words
that he was forced to interrupt himself
and leave the room that he might 1 give
way to his emotion. Both Judges and
others of the party felt the stress of the
moment, and when finally the oath which
made him President of the United States
had been completed. General Arthur
I sank into a chair, juried his face in his
â¢ lui nds and wjys thoroughly overcome.

Tidings Transmitted Through
the United States
Embassadors.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 14.â When the
blow fell and the official announcement
came that President McKinley had passed
away at 2:15 o'clock the crowds wh'ch
had been on the streets restlessly and sor
rowfully awaiting news of the end hart
retired for the night, as had all the Gov
ernment officials, save a few clerks at the
State, War and Navy departments. Sec
retary' Hay had given directions what
should, be done and Acting Chief Martin
and other employes, as soon as they re
ceived official confirmation of the news,
immediately sent cablegrams to each and
every United States Embassador and
Minister, or to the Charge where the
chief diplomatic officials were absent
from their posts, notifying them that
President McKinley died at 2:15 o'clock
this morning in Buffalo and instructing
them to inform the Governments to which
they were accredited.
There were no details in the message
nothing but its brief announcement â and
they were identical in language, except in
the names of the persons addressed.
The Embassadors t and Ministers were
expected to communicate the information
in turn to" the United States Consular of
ficers within the limits of their posts.. In
cases of countries like Australia and
Canada, where the United States Govern
ment is not represented in a diplomatic
capacity, messages of like character were
eent to the United States Consuls Gen
eral, who were to repeat them to the Con
suls.
The Â¦ original message was signed by
Secretary Hay, Mr. Babcock, his private
secretary, having taken it from the State
Department to the. Secretary's home for
that purpose. This was all that the De
partment of State could do to-night. As
r.oon as possible a proclamation will .be
Issued, announcing the President's demise
to the United States and the world at
large and- directing the closing of the
executive departments and - navy Â¦ yards
and army posts as a mark of mourning;
but this proclamation requires the signa<
ture of President Roosevelt and it. must
be countersigned also by Secretary Hay.
Arrangements have been made by which
Secretary Hay will meet acting Secretary
of War Gillespio and acting Secretary of
the Navy Hackett in the State Depart
ment in the morning for the purpose of
promulgating thÂ«5 necessary orders of the
three departments. The order of the War
Department has been prepared already.
It is drafted on lines' similar to that is
sued when President Garfleld wan strick
en. It had been . telegraphed to Secretary
Root for his approval and may be Issued
in his name.

Incidents of the Presidents
.Last Day Which Ends
in Despair.
9
MILBURN HOUSE, BUFFALO, Sept.
14.â The President's last day, which ended
in despair, was begun in hope. The ills
that came on Friday afternoon when the
organs of digestion refused to handle the
solid food that had been taken earlier in
the day had seemingly been overcome by
midnight and when the new day came it
found the President relieved and resting.
Hope, that had suddenly dropped from
the high place which it had held, began
to revive. The healing of the v/ounds had
progressed favorably, general conditions
were, in the main quite satisfactory and
the immediate future of the case seemed
to hold no threat. The physicians who
had been in almost constant attendance
during the night parted, and the watch
in the sick room was reduced.
Suddenly there was a failure of the
heart which for several days had been
manifesting slgnsi of weakness, and the
President sank toward unconsciousness.
This was at 2 o'clock in the morning.
There was an immediate application of
restoratives and a general call went out
to the absent physicians and nurses. Digi
talis, strychnine and saline solution were
administered to the patient, but there
was no immediate response to the treat
ment. The physicians admitted that ne
was desperately ill. and Secretary Cortol
you decided to send for the relatives and
close friends of the President, Vice Presi
dent and the members of the Cabtn.u.
Those within reach were called by tele
phone or messenger and telegrams were
rushed to those who had left the city.
The first of the messages were sent out
at 2:30 o'clock, and within half an hour
the Mllburn house began to nil again.
The serious condition of the President
and the general call sent out gave rise to
a' general feeling of alarm Jhat was never
again allayed. Desperate measures were
resorted to in order to stimulate the heart
and the sinking spell was over by 4
o'clock. It was decided to continue the
treatment, and the physicians laid their
greatest hope on weathering the day. " : It
was agreed that if the wounded -..'man
could be carried for twenty-four hours his
chances would be very favorable, for the
wounds were healing splendidly. .
It was decided to summon Dr.' W. W.
Johnston of Washington and Dr. E. 6.
Janoway of New York, heart specialists,
and telegrams were hurried out asking
that they come at once. Before dawn -a
desen of the relatives and friends of the
President arrived at the Mllburn house.
They assembled in the' drawing room,
where they waited for tidings from the
sick room above them. The physicians
nssurcd them- that the; Prexldent had a
fighting chnnco for his life, and to the
hope that In the' end victory would be his
they clung all day. "
Emma Goldman
Not Sorry for
MoKinley.
Sees Flag Lowered,
but Is Unmoved
by Sight.
4
Arch-Anarchist Uses Ven
omous Words in Chi
cago Jail.
>
Special Dispatch, to Tbe Call. '"
CHICAGO, Sept. 11â From the win
dow of her room In the Harrison
street-police station annex Rmma
Goldman saw the flag on the Cus
toms building flutter down to
half-mast, announcing premature
ly the "death of President McKln
ley. A' smile as of triumph flashed over
her face, but the next moment her coun
tenance resumed its immobility.
"Father in. heaven. Miss. Goldman," said
Matron Kegan, "the President Is dead.
Look at that flag and weep."
"Suppose he is dead," replied the apos
tle of anarchy. "Thousands die daily and
are upwept. "Why should I mourn ?"-
Near her the matron stood with gleam
ing eyes. The attendants in the room un
covered their, heads and gazed ruefully at
the flag. , The prisoners in an adjoining'
room to whom the news had been com
municated stood In dejected groups.
Emma Goldman alone stood unmoved.
"You officials mourn as a matter of
course," she said, with a flash of scorn
at the group.
"If I were in your position I might do
the same. The President was an ordinary
man, however, and I lament his loss no
more than that of. an ordinary laborer.
Would you weep if a common toiler wero
dead? No. Why then should I weep be
cause the President is dead?"
She crossed the room and took her seat
in a chair. Stoutly she rocked to and fro,
her lips compressed and her face set.
"No, I do not think the nation has sus
tained a loss," she said a minute later.
"1 am sorry- for Mr. McKinley as a man
and deplore his death as a human being,
but that is as far as my sympathy ex
tends."

All Washington Is
Shocked by the
Sad News.
? â Â¦
Hay Prepares to As
sume New Re
sponsibilities.
It Is Believed President
Roosevelt Will Make
Few Changes.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 13.â Th<*
news of the expected death
of President McKinley came
as a crushing blow to the
nation's capital. Nowhere,
perhaps, had the citizens
been so full of confidence In
the recovery of the President, and the
buoyant bulletins of the past week from
the sickbed had lulled them Into a false
sense of security, â¢which made the shock
terrible when the news that the Buffalo
tragedy would have a fatal ending came
to them.
All day long the bulletin boards were
surrounded by crowds waiting for the lat
est word from the Mllburn house. Little
work was done in the great executive de
partments, as at the slightest excuse the
clerks dropped their pens and turned to
talk of the chances that their chief mag
istrate had against death in the gallant
struggle he was making in the far-away
cltj- on the lake.
The three Cabinet officers in the capital
to-day were pictures of distress. Two
days ago Secretary Hay had left his chief
apparently on the road to recovery, and
to-day Postmaster General Smith, who
had left Buffalo even later and reached
here this morning, said when he came
from the Mllburn house yesterday that
the President was, according to his best
information, surely on the mend.
Burden on Hay's Shoulders.
The Postmaster General returned to
Buffalo on the 7;15 o'clock train to-night.
Secretary Hay had engaged accommoda
tions on to-night's New England train,
intending to return to his New Hamp
shire summer home for much-needed rest,
but canceled the order early in the day,
es on him devolved Important functions
at once on the President's demise. He
divided his time between the State De
partment and his home, waiting for the
end, prepared for his sorrowful duty or
conveying to the nations of the world in
official form the news of the demise of
the third American President through the
agency of an assassin's bullet wlthm the
short space of one human life.
As the senior member in rank of the
Cabinet, upon Mr. Hay's shoulders also
will rest the burden of the government of
the great republic In the short but Im
portant interval that must elapse before
the Vice President, under the terms of
the constitution, assumes the cares and
responsibilities of the Presidential office.
At his home In Woodley Secretary Gage
mournfully awaited the close of his chief's
life. During the day he had been at the
Treasury Department and had kept in
touch with financial affairs In order that
the President's end might not cause any
commercial disturbance that foresight on
the part of his department could avert.
General Glllespie, Acting Secretary of
War. - remained at his post in the War
Department to make such army orders as
the sad event would force him to issue.
He got into communication with General
Miles at Seattle and the latter notified the
department that he would return im
mediately.
"Will Tender Resignations.
The fact that the physicians had given
.up all hope caused some discussion among
the public men in the city of the prob
ability of an extra session of Congress
and of early changes in the Cabinet but
â¢ the consensus of opinion was that no Cab
inet changes or important departures in
public policy were at all likely for some
time to come and that Congress would not
be assembled until Its regular session in
December. All the Cabinet, the chief dip
lomatic officials and heads of the impor
tant bureaus, according to custom will
tender their resignations to relieve the
i President of embarrassment, but It is be
lieved Mr. Roosevelt would follow the
precedent of President Arthur after Gar
field's death and allow them to continue
in office. Some of the Cabinet officers are
not in good health and would sincerely
welcome a relief from their offices, but all
would waive their personal desires and
continue until such time as they could be
relieved easily. If indeed they request re
lief at all.
Under the Presidential succession act a
Cabinet officer becoming chief magistrate
would have to summon Congress in special
session; but this requirement does not
apply to a Vice President succeeding to
the office of his predecessor and It is be
lieved no extra session would be called in
the absence of statutory requirements as
the three mbnths intervening until De
cember can be bridged over without diffi
culty and there is no special occasion for
Congress assembling.

PLEA IS MADE
FOR ANARCHISTS
Nine Suspects Are Brought
Into Criminal Court at
Chicago.
CHICAGO, Sept. 13.â Hearing on _ the
writ of habeas corpus issued Wednesday
for the release of the anarchists under
arrest here began before Judge Chetlin in
the Criminal Court at 11:40 a. m. to-day^.
Judge Chetlin arrived at 11:30 a. m., and
a few minutes later the nine . prisoners
charged with conspiracy to kill the Presi
dent were led In and given seats behind
a row of bailiffs. Sheriff Magerstadt was
present. Throughout the room were Dep
uty Sheriffs and detectives in plain
clothes, the Sheriff having determined to
take every possible precaution against
trouble.
The writ of habeas corpus having been
complied with in the production of the
nine prisoners in court, counsel then made
returns in behalf of the Sheriff, the Chief
of Police and Justice Prindeville, who,
according to the petition, held the prison
ers under restraint Illegally. The prison
ers brought into court were: Abraham
Isaak, Abraham Isaak Jr., Clemens
Pfuetzner, Hippolyte Havel, Henry Tra
vagllo. Michael Roze.'Martin Roznick and
Morris J. Fox. Emma Goldman was pres
ent, but Justice Prindeville had promised
to follow whatever decision was made by
Judge ChetliÂ». - â¢ - '
Leopold Saltiel. representing the prison
ers, opened the argument, asking for the
release* of his clients on the ground that
they were being held without a shadow
of legal reason.. Attorney Saltiel claimed
that. all the papers in the case were-in
sufficient and were issued under false pre
tense. John F. Geetlng, assistant counsel
for the prisoners, followed Saltiel. Â¦
When Lawyer Geetlng ceased speaking.
Dr. Taylor, for the city, arose andsald
the. only question before the court ; was
the adequate Jurisdiction of the Justice's
Courtâ that Is, the Jurisdiction of Magis
trate. Prindeville. His ; argument , was
technical, tending to uphold the authority
of the lower court and the. legality of the
official acts of Magistrate Prindevillc.
The case was then postponed until 10
o'clock to-morrow morning, no decision
being given on any of the points raised.
City . Prosecutor Taylor, and his assist
ants were in secret conference this morn
ing concerning the habeas corpus case.
Taylor refused absolutely to say whether
any evidence against the anarchist sus
pects had been secured. Chief of Police
O'Neill said that as far as he knew no
evidence had been produced by the Buf
falo authorities; but no matter, what ac
tion^ the court might take the public could
rest\ assured that the police would keep
an eye on the anarchists, so as to be
ready for any contingency.
New Law "Will Probably Deal; With
Assailants of Presidents. , -
WASHINGTON, Sept.- 13.â Messrs. Bot
kin and Bynum, ; members : of 'the â¢ com
mission to revise and codify; the criminal
and penal laws , of " the United States,
since the attack upon the life of the Pres-
ident have been investigating the author
ities Â¦with a view, to the preparation of a
law making an assault upon the Presi
dent of the United States with the intent
to murder cognizable to the courts of the
United States and punishable by death.
They have prepared a draft of a bill to
be submitted to Congress, making assault
upon the President punishable by death
when the assault is for the purpose of
obstructing the operations. of the Govern
ment. It. is believed that this qualifica
tion will have the effect of giving the
Federal courts jurisdiction in such cases.

DOCTORS MAY
HOLD AUTOPSY
Anxious to Determine the
Exact Cause of Presi
dent's Death.
MILBTJRN HOUSE, BUFFALO, Sept.
14.â It. is almost certain that an autopsy
on the President's body will be held to
determine the exact cause of death. This
is the wish of all the surgeons and physi
cians who were In attendance. The pecu
liar action of the President's heart was
more or less of a puzzle to all, and Dr.
McBurney expressed the opinion that in
the interest of science as well as the in
terest of the Government and the world
it was desirable that the exact cause of
death be determined.. .
The President's heart gave trouble from
the beginning, but its erratic action was
at first believed to be due to the shock
of the wound. When the wound had be-
I gun to progress favorably the heart gave
more trouble and anxiety than ever. Its
action became feeble and finally gave out
altogether.. . . â¢ Â¦
The theory of at least one of. the physi
cians is that the original shock caused by
the first wound over, the heart had much
to do with the trouble which caused death.

GHIGAflO, Sept. 13.â "If the

wherever she can be found.
'"CHARLES WETMLAND, No. 555
West Monroe street.
"JOHN IRVING FEARCE Jr.,
Sherman House.'
"EDWARD MYERS, No. 248 Dear
born avenue.
This startling appeal was circulated
through the city and sent to the newspa
per offices to-night. The three mfcn who
sign the proclamation calling on Chicago
to take the initiative in ridding the coun
try of anarchists and asking its citizens
to summarily take the law in their own
hands declare they did not issue it In a
spirit of bravado, but with the sober ex
pectation that it would meet with a re
sponse. Each avers he- would willingly
lead an avenging mob.
"There are times when the people should
take the law into their own hands, and
when codes and precedents are not suffi
cient," said J. Irving Pearce Jr. of the
Sherman House. "The present Is one of
those times. We want an expression of
the wish of the people, and offer them
an opportunity to make a demonstration.
Anarchy should be met by its own weap
ons. Emma Goldman, the arch conspira
tor, is the person whom we especially
wish to secure and give her a taste of
the beheading which she advocates. "When
we issued the proclamation- we meant
every, word we said, and I would volun
teer to be one of the leaders in avenging
the death of President McKinley."
Rumors of mob violence this evening
Â¦were the cause of much concern at cen
tral police headquarters and In the de
tective bureau. On the streets opinions
were freely expressed that the public
should take a hand in dealing with an
archists.
After receiving alarming reports from
the streets, Chief O'Neill issued an order
holding 100 detectives in readiness to move
at a moment's notice on the County Jail
or the Harrison-street police station in
case public sentiment grew so strong that
the ordinary police details could not pro
tect the imprisoned anarchists.

sorrow exhibited Is as that for a personal
friend. - .

men's Association to-night, but instead of
speaking upon the relation of the laity
and the clergy, as He Intended, he spoke
of President McKinley.
"For," said he, "whatever we may talk
of there is only one thought in all our
hearts to-night."
He dwelt upon the grand life of the
President as man and statesman and the
gTeat love Â¦which the nation had for him.
And he gave personal reminiscences of
McKinley, the soldier, in a voice that
trembled with deep emotion.
. The feeling among Methodists here, both
clergy and laity, against Czolgosz and
anarchy in general is intense and some
organized effort against anarchy in Cali
fornia will, it. is said, result from it.

THE 'CORONER ARRIVES.

dent's breathing was. .barely .percep
j tible. His pulse had practicatly -ceased

recognized that nothing remained but

house.

ival of the Coroner pave rise to one
such rumor, and a flood of groundless

NORTH CREEK, N. Y.. Sept It-
President Roosevelt arrived at
Aiden Lair at 3:15 a, m. The six
teen miles between that place
and this village will be covered
in the quickest possible time. A
relay of horses has been provtdad
in order that he may reach North Creek,
where his special train awaits him.
The roads are in a. very poor condition
on account of the heavy rainfall of the.
past two days and bnt slow progress can
be made. If nothing happens to prevent,
he should arrive here about 5 o'clock.
Mf. Roosevelt is now within reach of
telephone communication, but has not
been informed of President McKinley s
death, and will not be until he arrives in
North Creek.
Banger Attends the B-ide.
The road, to be traveled, from Aiden
Lair to this place is through heavy forest
timber, and the trip will be attended by
more or less danger. The roads are very
narrow in many places, with deep ravines
on either side. A slight deviation would
mean a. broken carriage or perhaps more
serious trouble. â
Mr. Roosevelt, on his arrival here, will
at once take the special train for Albany,
arriving there about 7 a. m. At Albany
ai.cther special will be awaiting to convey
him' over the New York Central Railroad
direct to Buffalo.
ALBANY, Sept. 12.â Vice President
Roosevelt reached the camp of the Ta

five miles north of North Creek, at 9
o'clock. He was found on the top of
Mount Warcy. where he had gone on a
hunting expedition, by guides who had
been sent out early in the day to deliver
to him the telegram from Buffalo an
nouncing a change for the worse -in the
President's condition.
Reaches Albany This Morning.
At 9:45 o'clock to-night Superintendent
Hammond of the Delav/are and Hudson,
who has been at North Creek all day with
a special train to bring Vice President
Roosevelt to Albany, telegraphed the

velt could not possibly reach North Creek
before 5 or 6 o'clock in the morning.
Mr. Hammond also wired to Superin
tendent Harrington of this division of the
New Tork Central and Hudson River

possibly reach Albany before 7 or 8
S o'clock to-morrow rooming. The Dela

DEATH IS ANNOUNCED.
It was now 2:05 o'clock and

rest. A moment more and Dr.

"The President is dead." ',

No Time for Partisanship.
Later, when the committee of the Car-,
ter if. Harrison League, at whose picnic
Mr. Bryan was to have, spoken to-mor
row, announced that the demonstration
had been abandoned, Mr. Bryan, In a
voice that shook with emotion, read the
following:
"I most cordially commend your action
In abandoning the contemplated demon
stration. We are .all oppressed by the
grief which has fallen upon the nation.
We have no spirit for a partisan meeting
at this time, for partisamship is swallo wetl
up in universal sorrow. Political contro
versies and the prizes and disappoint
ments which attend them, dwindle into
insignificance Â¦ when we stand In the pres
ence of the tragedy which seems likely to
rbb the nation of its chief executive. We
all feel the humiliation that our country
has suffered and our hearts are with the
lovely woman from whom foul and brutal
assassination is taking one of the moat
faithful and tender of husbands. Whether
the heroic struggle ends fatally or in his
restoration to health, you have acted
wisely."

7THE SAN FRANCISCO GALL,, SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 14, 1901.

CITY'S EXPRESSION OF SORROW
MAYOR PHELAN SUGGESTS THAT ALL
BUSINESS HOUSES BE CLOSED TODAY
ON RECEIPT of the news of the President's death the following statement was made by Mayor
Phelan:
"Words fail to express my grief and sorrow at the calamity that ha befallen the nation. I
will make a suggestion to the people of this city to=morrow morning that all places of business be closed
for the day as a mark of sympathy and sorrow at the nation's loss. I will also suggest that the places
of amusement in the city also close in the afternoon and evening.
" On Monday the Board of Supervisprs meet and we will then take steps to observe a day of
mourning in the city. I cannot state now just what these plans might be, but I will favor public serv=
ices on the day of the President's funeral and such other marks of respect and sympathy as will be ap
propriate. The death of the President will be acutely felt in San Francisco, for he endeared himself to
thousands during his recent sojourn with us. The hearts of our citizens will go oujt in sympathy to the be=
reaved wife and relatives of our martyred President. JAMES D. PHELAN."
CITIZENS SILENTLY
PRAY FOR RECOVERY
OF STRICKEN CHIEF
FIFTY-EIGHT TAPS
RING OUT NEWS OF
PRESIDENT'S DEATH
MESSAGE OF DEATH
RECEIVED WITH DEEP
SORROW AND GRIEF.
The Mournful Dispatch Read in
Silence by Crowds on
the Streets.
THE DREAD fire alarm bells were trans
muted into mournful taps last ni^ht soon
after the news of President McKinley's
death reached the city. -_ Fifty-eight times the
bells rang out in the still of the midnight
air, tolling out the age of the country's dead
executive.
As soon as the information reached Chief
Sullivan of President McKinley's death he or
dered the bells rung. The streets were not yet
cleared of the anxious crowds that stood in
waiting, round r the bulletin 'boards hour after
hour. At first it was thought to be an alarm of
fire, but after the tenth bell had struck the sor
rowing public realized its import.
Anxious Crowds Gather at Call
Offices and Wait for
Bulletins.
S~m^ AN FRANCISCO . was thrown
A^ ' into deep gloom and sorrow yes
*^S^ tcrday when her citizens learned
. ' of the critical condition of the
J^ / President.
' The early extra editions of
The CalK telling of the sudden
chsnge for the worse In the President's
condition, were the medium of carrying
the sad news to thousands of* the city's
residents, and during: the entire day all
personal and business cares gave way to
the one vital question, "Will the beloved
President live?"
In front, of the business office of The
Call In the Claus Spreckels building, hun
dreds of men, women and children gath
ered during the morning and at the lunch
hour the waiting crowd grew into' thou
sands.
From everj' quarter of the city tele
phone messages were sent to the offices
of The Call, anxiously asking as to the
President's condition.
-In business circles, in offices. In the
City Hall and Federal building, on the
streets and on the cars, the crisis in the
condition . of the President was the sole
topic of conversation.
Sadness, was depicted on the faces of
men and women and the many thousands,
who a few weeks ago gave hearty wel
come to McKinley, felt they were almost
at the bedside of the dying man.
In. front of The Call office, the bulletins
were received in grim silence. Those who
waited hour after hour for the latest
news from the East breathed silent pray
ers that, the Ruler of the Universe might
spare the nation's chief executive and
assuage the grief of the republic.
Erroneous Dispatch Causes Grief.
In the afternoon when a dispatch from
Buffalo . was received announcing the
President's death a thrill of horror went
through the entire city. Flags were set
at halfmast and many of the leading re
tail and wholesale h.ouses closed their
places of business.
Grief was turned to joy, however, when
later on the telegraphic news from the
East was that the President still lived.
At (J p. m. . the crowd in front of The
Call office was estimated at thousands
and the cable cars were blocked in their
progress, along Market street.
Considerable excitement was caused
when a scoundrel, whose name is un
known, declared in loud tones that "more
men like. McKinley should be murdered."
"Long". John Wllkins, the colored politi
cian, heard the speech of the disciple of
anarchy and asked the fellow if he meant
what he had said. "Every word of it,"
\vas the reply, as the speaker made a mo
tion to draw a pistol from his hip pocket.
Wilkins at once smashed the- man over
the bead Â¦ with a heavy cane and was ar
rested by. Officer RIley, who witnessed the
occurrence. In the excitement the de
famer of the President made his escape In
the crowd. Wilkins was charged with
Lattery but was promptly bailed out by
Attorney Horace Ford.
Anxiously Wait for Bulletins.
The crowd in front of The Call's offices
increased rapidly, and by 8 p. m. a solid
mass of humanity was closely packed to
prether at MarkeUand Third streets. The
lrequent bulletins that told of the battle
with death were eagerly read, but there
was a dread feeling that all hope had
pone v.'hen the messages told of the un
conscious state of the President. '
The dispatch announcing the arrival of
Dr. Janeway. the noted heart specialist,
at the' bedside oi the President caused the
drooping spirits of San Francisco's citi
zens to revive. Expressions were heard
on all sides in the crowd of thousands
ihat perhaps the specialist might save the
spark of life that was flickering.
Men distinguished in all walks of life
/gathered In. the various clubs and dis
cussed the latest news from the dying
President's bedside. With the deep sym
pathy expressed were also heard denun
ciations of the yellow press, that had by
vilifying the chief executive brought
from their dark lairs the cowardly band
of murderers, whose representative had
struck at a nation's heart.
_When the theaters and other places of
.amusement closed last evening many of
the audiences wended their way to The
Call's business office to learn of the con
dition of the President.
The various cable and electric cars car
ried the news of the bulletins of the Pres
ident's condition to the residents of the
outlyirii? districts. The gripmen, motor
men and conductors were asked for'in
formation at each street corner, and those
who questioned them carried the news to
their neighbors on streets adjacent to the
car lines. _.
Old Friends Express Sympathy.
At a meeting of the Old Friends the fol
lowing dispatch was sent to President Mc-
Kinley:
The "Old Friends." of which society you are
an honorary member, extends to you the sym
pathy of its members, as President of the
United States, as well as a brother of our so
ciety, in the late dastardly attempt on your
life. JUDGE CHAS. T. CONLAN, President.
J. B. SLINKET, Secretary.
ITALIAN EDITORS FIGHT
ABOUT ANARCHISTS
Ferruccio Pedretti Is Arrested for
Battery on Complaint of
Ettore Patrizi.
Ettcre Patrizi, editor of 1/ It alia, -swore
to a complaint in Judge Cabaniss' court
yesterday, charging Ferruccio Pedretti,
editor of La Voce del Popoio, with bat
tery. Pedretti was arrested by Detectives
Whittaker and McMahon and booked at
the City Prison. He was released on $50
bail.
Patrizi, in the columns of his paper, has
been denouncing the anarchists- for the
assassination of the President, and In yes
terday morning's edition accused Pedretti
of not having the courage to give utter
ance in his paper to his abhorrence of the
dastardly deed.
"This afternoon about 2 o'clock," said
Patrizi, "I was standing in front of my
office, talking to a friend, when Pedretti
walked from behind my friend and struck
me three blows on the face. I was dazed
for a moment by the unexpected attack,
and by that time Pedretti had fled."
Pedretti's story is that L'ltalia had
been constantly insinuating that he was
the friend of anarchists and he offered to
donate $500 to a charity if one word in his
paper could be shown bearing out the as
sertion, but the challenge was not accept
ed. \JVhen he passed Patrizi on the street
Pedretti says his rival editor struck at
him, but he got in the first blow. Then he
walked away.
FIND FTTSE FOR BOMBS.
Police Discover Evidence in the Boom
of a Suspect.
CLEVELAND, Sept. 13â In a frame
building on Broadway the detectives have
discovered evidence which, while not
tending to prove the existence of an an
archist plot against President McKinley
will at least serve to hold one of the men
arrested In Chicago." â¢
As soon as information was received
here of the arrest of Edward Wolcinynski
of this city in Chicago Sergeant Doran
and Detective Schmunk were detailed to
search the house in which he had lived
at 1870 Broadway. They found stored
av.ay on top of a cupboard fifty feet of
fuse, such as is ordinarily used In the
manufacture of dynamite bombs. The in
formation was at once telegraphed to the
Chicago authorities.

MISTAKE BY OPERATOR.
Tremature Announcement of Presi
dent's Death Sent to White House.
WASHINGTON, Sept.- 13.â A premature
announcement of the death of President
McKinley was given out at the White
House by Chief Operator Montgomery
shortly before 6:30 o'clock and was re
called a few moments later. In explana
tion it was stated that the operator at the
Buffalo end of the White House wire had
been handed a bunch of messages an
nouncing the President's death and imme
diately began rushing them off. . He had
sent three of the messages when thev
were recalled and canceled and word
given to "kill" those sent.

FORMED AUARCHIST LODGES.
Czolgosz Reported to Have Been Ope
rating in Coal Eegipns.
WILKESBARRE, Pa., Sept. 13.â It was
learned here to-day that Leon Czolgosz
made a tour of the anthracite coal regions
about six years ago. ' He was employed
for a while by . the Lackawanna Coal
Company at Duryea, this county, and
whila there is said to have organized a
lodge of anarchists. He could speak sev
eral languages and soon became popular
with the foreign element. He was known
as Fred Neiman.
People who came in contact with Czol
gosz then say that he was after notoriety
all the time, and whenever the anarchists
met he Insisted on doing all the talking.
His theory was, that the condition of the
working people in this country would
never be improved until the doctrines ad
vocated by the anarchists' were put in
practice. ; â¢

Offer Song as a Prayer.
The students of the University of Cali
fornia, at a general meeting in the gym
nasium, arose this morning and sang the
first verse of "My Country, 'Tis of Thee,"
offering it as a prayer for the recovery
of the head of the nation. The verse
came . with great feeling from the .voices
of the students joined in supplication and
in hope. The verse was sung at the sug
gestion of President Wheeler, who, just
beforo dismissing the audience at- the
close of the regular address, said:
"When the. beloved head of our nation' lies at
the present crisis we can only feel our very
hearts' love Involved in the Issue. May I ask
you all to rise and sing one . verse of .. "My
Country 'Tis of Thee," offering if as a prayer
to God for the recovery of our chief executive?
THE message telling of the death
of the beloved President was. re
ceived, last night with the deepest
feelings of sorrow and grief.
The telegram announcing the
passing away of the chieftain was
received at The Call's offices at
11:20 p. m., five minutes after, the sad
event.
A large crowd was gathered in front
of the business offices of The Call and
for some minutes before the final bulletin
was posted a deathlike stillness prevaded
the assemblage. Not a sound could be
heard save the rumble of a passing car or
vehicle.
All eyes were fixed on The Call attache
who, brush In hand, was waiting to print
on the white canvas the latest news x'rom
the bedside of the dying chief. . â¢ .
A member of The Call's staff suddenly
appeared with a written telegraphic mes
sage and hundreds of men and women
watched with sorrow the printing of the
fatal, "The President is dead." Not a
word was spoken and not a comment wa3
made, as with bowed heads and blanched
faces the loyal citizens turned away from
the spot where they had waited for hours
hoping and praying for a message of
cheering . news.
Homeward they wended their way, with
hearts filled with grief over the nation's
loss, a loss that adds another name to the
glorious roll of. those who have died for
their country.

The' State Board of the Ancient Order

lowing resolutions in memory of Presl-
dent McKinley:
"Whereas, The Ancient Order of Hibernians,
true to its principles and constitution, has al
ways enjoined en its members loyalty and de
votion to the country of their adoption. It haÂ»
held up as examples worthy of admiration an<
reverence the careers of many noble Irishmen
who. from the revolution down to the late war,
gave up their lives in battle for the Union.
It has taught that love for this country should
be equal with the love for mother Ireland, and
Whereas, The President of the United States
has fallen a victim to an assassin's shot and
an attempt has thus been made on the Â¦ Gov
ernment and institutions of the country we de
sire to record our abhorence of the murderous
assault, to express our heartfelt sympathy at

our sincere respect and affection, and we pray
that God in his wisdom may afford consola
tion to Mrs. McKinley In this sad hour of her
great â¢ loss and desolation. Be it .
Resolved, That these resolution be entered
on our â¢ minutes and that they be conveyed
to the family of our martyred President. Be
it further
Resolved. That our charter be draped In
mourning for a period of thirty days.

The announcement of the death of the
President last night resulted In a meet
ing of the Theatrical Managers' Associa
tion for the purpose of taking some action
in the premises. It was decided to close
all theaters this afternoon and evening
As was anticipated the audiences at the
various theaters were very small last
night. The Columbia had a good advance
sale, but many of the seats paid for
during the week were not used. At the
Central a decided drop in the attendance
was observable. The Alcazar was well
filled, but Manager Thall was prepared
up to 10 o'clock to dismiss his audience
immediately on receipt of news from Buf
falo and to refund the money. The Or
pheum, ordinarily crowded to the doors
presented rows upon rows of unoccupied
seats. . â¢
The box sale of seats at all the theaters
was extremely slow and no effort was
made to force matters. The theater
goers of this city, bowed down as they
were with the grief that now shakes the
JVfart of the nation, preferred to study
the bulletins which told the story of the
tragedy of a human life cut short in its
prime, to witnessing mimetic representa
tion of any description. Where hearts
are full of grief and anxiety mirth or thÂ«
semblance of it finds no room.

FATE OF THE PRESIDENTS
THREE PRESIDENTS OF THE UNITED
STATES HAVE BEEN ASSASSINATED
PRESIDENT ABRAHAM LINCOLN died at 22 minutes after 7 o'clock on the morning of
Saturday April 15, 1865. He was shot by John Wilkes Booth.
President James A. Garfield died on Monday, September I9, 1881, at 35 minutes
after iO in the evening. He was shot by Charles J. Guiteau.
President William HcKinley died on Saturday, September i4, 1001, at 2:15 o'clock in the
morning. He was shot by Leon Czolgosz.

radical remedy had its effect and the
movement of ihe bowels came, bringing
with it an Immediate lowering pulse and
great relief.

BUFFALO. Sept. 13.â Large crowds
have assembled on Main street in the vi
cinity of the newspaper offices and on the
streets suroundlng police headquarters
where Colzgosz is confined. Although
the sidewalks are so crowded that pedes.
trians are forced to walk in tho center of
the streets tho throng is an orderly one.
and no demonstration against the prisoner
has taken place. As a precautionary
measure ropes have been stretched across
all Btreets leading to police headquarters,
and no one is allowed to approach the sta-
tlon except newspaper men. A large fsree
of police is on duty about the building,
and one approaching is challenged at
every twenty or thirty feet. A reserve
force is held in the headquarters building
for any emergency that may arise. The
crowd Is a particularly quiet one, every
person silentlv and eagerly scanning the
bulletin boards for some word of hope
from the Milburn house.

NORTH CREEK, N. Y., Sept. 13, 8:30

where Mr. Roosevelt Is stopping, with the
latest bulletins, showing the alarming
condition of the President. These should
reach him at 10 p. m. If he should start
at once upon their receipt he will be
obliged to drive or ride thirty-five miles
over dark roads before the village of
North Creek can be reached.
Arrangements have been made to re
ceive word here as soon as he passes the
lower clubhouse.

relinquished to the city by the United
States by act of Congress.

COFFEE DISEASES.
Minister and _ Young I.ady Affected.
Ministers sometimes find they suffer
from the effects of bad habits as well as
ordinary people. Rev. Mr. of Ath-
en*, N. Y., had become greatly emaciated
from coffee drinking:, which produced
etomach trouble and all of the effects of
oveVwork or poor nourishment.
He quit the coffee and began drinking
Postum Cereal Food Coffee.
His health began to Improve and he
now weighs 151 pounds, an Increase of IS
pounds over his former weight. This Im-
provement In health and strength Is
shown to be due to the use of Postum
Food Coffee, by the fact that when he
stops drinking Postumâ as he has done
for an experimentâ he begins to lose flesh
and get back Into his old condition.
A young lady who writes about the case
says that she was formerly suffering
gTeatly from "those twin diseases." dys-
pepsia and nervousness. "I knew that
both of the diseases had their origin In
the use of coffee, and while I was' fully
aware of its Injurious effects upon my
system I was not willing to give it up,
for I did not know of anything to take its
place.
"Tea, I knew, was also Injurious, and
as for cocoa. It lacked the 'snap and go'
which can alone satisfy a coffee drinker's
taste. About two years ago I purchased
my first box of Postum Food Coffee and
quit the use of coffee. I made Postum
according to directions and found I had
a drink not only equal to coffee but far
superior to It In many ways. Since that
time I have used it constantly and find
my general health very much improved
and the 'twin diseases' gone. I also send
you the names of Rev. and Mrs. of
Athens, N. Y., who have been greatly
helped by the use of Postum Food Coffee
in the" place of ordinary coffee."

THE EMBATTLED FARMERS.
rp^HE Farmers' Convention at Sacramento on
I Thursday fired a resolution that was heard
Â¦*â¢ around the State. The pressure of their neces
sities brought them together to see whether there is
any government that can save their crops from de
struction by protecting, in their lawful right to la
bor, the workmen who are willing to handle those
crops on their way to market. They appointed a
committee to begin at the beginning, at the first
point- where the law is executed, the county Sheriff
in Contra Costa. If he cannot execute the law ap
peal goes to the Governor. If that official, through
the failure of the county authorities to call on him,
cannot interfere, the farmers, thrown upon their own
resources, will repair to the shipping point and with
arms protect the transit of their property to market.
The labor leaders and the Examiner purposely
promoted the congestion of perishable property at
the point of shipment, in the expectation that it
would bring public opinion among the farmers to
the aid of the strikers. <Jn this they signally failed.
The farmers saw abundant' labor offering to handle
their property in transit, and daily witnessed the use
of force and violence to deter and intimidate that
willing labor. _ They were themselves importuned by
strikers from the city for country employment at
less wages than the country rate. They saw la
boiers from the country who took employment in the
city at ruling wages waylaid, beaten brutally and
their pockets picked by city strikers. They knew of
such laborers being seized by overwhelming numbers
of strikers and their arms broken to prevent them
working at all. They saw every pretense _of humanity
and regard for the public welfare put forward by the
strike leaders and the Examiner proved to be hol
low hypocrisy by the daily act of the pretenders.
Seeing these things and feeling the prospective
loss of millions of dollars in . perishable property,
they refused to be made the victim of the Exam
iner's desire to get nickels by posing as the friend of
striking labor, and have taken their affairs into their
flwn hands. When bad men conspire, good men com
bine. The Examiner and the strike leaders have
conspired, and the farmers have combined. We
have no doubt that they will succeed. The conges
tion at Port Costa will cease. Wheat â¢ will move.
The idle ships will load and sail away, and the farm
ers will learn the- value of doing their own think
ing and working together in their own interests.
Had this strike succeeded and had there been
established the right of the imported leaders
of labor to dictate what man's goods may go
to market and what rot in the field or on the
wharf, the next victims of their autocratic dictation
would have been the farmers, and we -would have
seen the seal of their displeasure put upon "unfair"
wheat, potatoes, melons or . peaches, and those ar
ticles left to rot on the wharf or. boycotted in the
market. '{' '
The farmers have won a victory for free labor and
free capital. [~\< : '\ ']Â¦/'. .
DEAD.
Â¦.'.'.. FT^ â "" â : : â .-: ..â¢Â¦.;',- . : ' Â¦'
THE President's death, coming close upon the bright promise of his recovery, hurts
the good' heart of the people more than if the assassin's malice had effected its
purpose immediately. In the universal sorrow there is little to be said that is
.comforting. Our people have taken pride in the peculiarly American career
of their Presidents. Washington was the owner of' a large estate, but his life was that ot
a plain man, the planter's life of his time, and aside from' his work as a civil engineer and
his military experience, his interests were with the land, the crops and the markets and
his country. ...
. -The elder and younger Adams were more nearly patrician than any others of our
Presidents. Jefferson lived upon the income of his land, and Madison and Monroe were
men of means below the average of their, time. Jackson was orphaned by the Revolution
in his childhood. His mother died by the wayside on her way from nursing the
wounded Continentals, and while in the Presidency he was concerned in the marketing of
the crops produced, and the skins of wild animals killed, on the lands around the Her
mitage. Martin Van Buren's life began among humble surroundings at Kiriderhook, and
both the Harrisons had rugged struggles in early life. Lincoln and Johnson had no her
itage but their genius. Grant began on ah Ohio farm. Garfield was a -barefoot boy on
the canal, and Cleveland had his growth upward through a weight of poverty and
privation. .'.-'Â¦'
To this class belonged William McKinley. The first necessity of his' life was self
support, and the Civil War found him a youth contributing cheerfully as a breadwinner
to the typically American family into which he was born. The tocsin stirred him to inime '-'
diate action, and he entered the ranks as a private soldier, offering his .life for that of his
country, to witness the victory of the Union as a commissioned officer. Â¦ '..['.
In*- Congress, as Governor of his State, and as President, he was always the typical
American.. On him no experience was lost. His early privations, his observations as an
industrial doctrinaire, his keen analysis of the conditions of popular prosperity, formed in
him the resolution to do his might for the industrial financial and commercial supremacy
of his country, to the end that not only should opportunity be equal and better than in
his youth, but that the means of men to grasp it should be increased. Dominated.by this
idea, his election to the Presidency made him the instrument of its realization.
He has fallen in the midst of his fruition among men. By .his fidelity his country
is the foremost nation. No empire past or present exceeded its power. He found it in
dustrially depressed and financially dependent. It mourns with his household, a nation
that, centers/within itself the power todictate to the world. â¢
The day that a wretched assassin executed upon him the malice of his detractors,
his country could boast the largest number of the best paid laborers, the most pros
perous farmers and manufacturers in the world, the most contented homes, the best fed
and best clothed people upon whom the sun shines. In all this his agency. was direct
and not intermediate. The benefactor of his country dies, murdered by a monster who
prates in justification his love of "the common people!" A typical American, the light of
the nation's life, is dead, slain by the distorted spawn of imported degeneracy, inspired by
a sentiment promoted by one who 'should- be outlawed by all races and all nations
and scorned by all men.
The nation will wipe away its tears and go forward. The great President survives
himself. The impulse his potent genius has given to the interests of the people, will con
tinue.. This is a land of law which all make, all must obey and none can assassinate. His
constitutional successor will take the great office and its great responsibilities, and hear
them like a man. % The assassin sought to murder a nation, but that nation will live with
power unimpaired.' It will fulfill 5 the high destiny devised for it by ; its stricken citizen
and chief. Its people will draw closer together. They will,- with awakened zeal, demand
that American life and energy shall be less polluted by the turbid stream that has poured
upon our shores, bringing the morbid and murderous blood that breeds. such reptiles as
this assassin. They will more quickly mark the degenerate ingrate among themselves,
whose cunning lies and false alarms misrepresent his country.. Over the white face of the
dead they will tafke s fresh oath of fidelity to freedom, to American principles, to a higher
sense of duty, and they will make this a land in which no traitor to the principles of its
people can live. * { ;
While the bells toll for^the dead and the somber emblems of mourning are
abroad, and the sun shines less brightly 'and men go softly and downcast, let no one mis
take it all for a sign of despair. It means heart-soreness and sorrow as the beginning of
a hjgh resolve that toleration- of the causes of this mighty crime has ended.
MUNICIPAL PATERNALISM.
THE convention of the political labor unionists
in this city has followed the example of the
charter makers by fefvoring municipal paternal
ism, through the public ownership and operation' of
public utilities. . â¢ \
It has been the desire of The Call to so discuss this
economic question as to permit the people who de
sire to embark this city in such business operations
to take the plunge open-eyed. We have developed
the in municipal â¢ paternalism elsewhere to a
demonstration that municipal waste exceeds corpor
ate profits in the administration of the same lines of
business. It is an interesting and suggestive fact
that wherever an effort is made to compel such pub
lic bookkeeping as- will disclose all items of expense
and income in the operation of business enterprises
under municipal ownership a determined opposition
appears, in which municipal officers and the theoret
ical advocates of such ownership â¢* appear to com
bine. â¢ '
The doctrinaires of , municipal paternalism never
lire of quoting the example of the British crown col
ony, New Zealand, which is cited as an economic
paradise and model of successful government under
highly communistic conditions. The London Times"
published last month a letter from that colony which
indicates that these -conditions are producing re
sults expected by practical men. . The Edinburgh
Scotsman, commenting on this, says that' the colony
treasury is empty and the Government is compelled
to borrow small sums at 4 per cent. Mr. -Scddon,
the New Zealand Premier, admits that. the Govern
ment lizs been unable" to make receipts equal to ex
penditures. /
A glance at the methods of that Government easily^
accounts, for this'. The 'Government is in the life
insurance business, owns the railways, and bought
great tracts of land on, which to . settle socialistic
colonies. It lias adopted a standard fate of wages
for all labor, regardless of. any natural law of supply
and demand/ It provides work for all unemployed.
The colony has borrowed a great deal of money to
carry on these experiments in 'paternalism, to' build
railroads" and furnish employment. The Scotsman
says that .all. these .public works cost double -what
they should. Nor was this , the v whole evil. ' The
money .spent in giving work to the unemployed vir
tually became a bribe for. votes for the Government.
Ministers bought the labor vote by the lavish distri
bution as % wages of the money they borrowed. It
became the policy of the Ministers to let every fad
dist carry his measure. When its futility was demon
strated it would be repealed. The compulsory arbi
tration act to settle labor disputes promoted" such
disputes and has caused a paralysis of business.
The colony is rich in natural resources, and, left
to the development of private enterprise, offers de
sirable opportunities. But the Government has re
pressed such enterprise. It has practically penalized
the private} ownership of land, and in the endeavor
to create a socialist heaven has made an industrial
hell.
Fortunately, these experiments in the United
States have been confined to cities and on a less
comprehensive scale. But they are no "more suc
cessful. The latest failure is that of public owner
ship of. the natural gas' plant in Toledo, Ohio. To
ledo is dominated by that primate of -socialism and
fads, "Golden Rule Jones," who has been its Mayor
for many, years.- Ten years ago that city issued a
million dollars in bonds to create a public gas plant.
Its cost of administration gradually increased until
the burden became unbearable to the taxpayers, and
it has been leased to a private corporation at an' an
nual rental of $6500. The interest charge on the
debt is $47,250, and the taxpayers have to make up
the difference between that and Jhe rental. The plant
had deteriorated, and the taxpayers were unwilling
to stand the cost of repairing the ravages of neglect
and bad management."
The Toledo Bee, commenting on the affair, says:
"Incidentally many- of the men who shouted loudest
for the public' gas plant got their start in life' through
their connection with its administration, and having
become taxpayers managed to, bob up in time to urge
the Council to lease it to a\ private corporation, so
as to save their taxes." J Â¦-.

The Columbia Which Is Again to Defend the Cup Was a
Strong Favorite Two Years Ago.
JN view of the near approach of the international yacht races for the Ameri
ca's cup, every one interested in the big contests, especially those who will
soon be making wagers on the outcome, will beÂ«glad to know how the bet
ting on-"the cup races in 1899 stood. It was strongly in favor of the Colum
bia, the early odds generally standing at 2 to 1. The form shown by the
Shamrock I In her trial spin changed the odds materially. Some of the largest
wagers were as follows:
Captain Lawrence, a friend of Lord Dunraven, bet $2500 to J200O on the
Shamrock I with Riley Grannan.
A prominent New York club member bet $1000 even on the Columbia.
"Tom" O'Rourke bet $1000 to $S00 on the Columbia.

Leo Swatts bet $600 to $1000 on the Shamrock I with a syndicate.

INGENIOUS DEVICES EMPLOYED
BY DESERTERS FROM THE ARMY
Dissatisfied Fighting Men Adopt Novel Plans for Abandon
ing the Ranks.
TO desert from the. army Is not the
easiest thing in the world, and even
when accomplished the culprit
lives in daily fear of being recap
tured and sentenced to a heavy term
of imprisonment. Therefore, the de
vices employed by some deserters are as
ingenious as they are exciting* although
they cannot be said to be always crowned
with success. - For sheer audacity the
case of an Irish soldier who deserted in a
south coast town in England some years
ago would be hard to beat. Just at that
time, a number of desertions had occurred
in the â¢ neighborhood and the authorities
were therefore unusually sharp to prevent
further offenses of this nature. But the
man in question was dissatisfied with sol
diering and having, a number of friends in
the town who were disposed to help him,
and being possessed of an unusually large
modicum of pluck, resolved to delay his
return to civil life no longer. One morn
ing early he slipped out of the barracks
and made his way to a friend's house
without being seen. Arriving there he
shaved off his mustache, and, donning a
policeman's uniform, boldly promenaded
the main streets of the town. He was,
of course, missed in the barracks and
knew that the* 'surrounding country was
being scoured In the hope of finding him.
j On one occasion his sergeant came up to
him and asked him if he had seen or
heard anything of the missing man, and
he, of course, replied that he had not," but
he would keep his eyes open! . For inore
than a fortnight he preserved his dis
guise without it being penetrated even by
the police themselves, and he then calm
ly left the town when the hue and cry had
subsided somewhat. Nevertheless, his en
terprise came to naught, for he was ar
rested in trying to work his passage back
to Ireland.
Scarcely less audacious was the ruse
employed by another dissatisfied son ot
Mars, and in this case, likewise, it proved
futile. He happened to know that a cer
tain publican in the town was In anything
but affluent circumstances, for he visited
the house regularly, and from remarks
dropped at odd times discovered that the
publican was heavily in debt to a Scotch
spirit merchant. > One morning, as soon
as the publican opened his doors, the sol
dier arrived, after having first disguised
himself with a beard and wig and donned
civilian attire. With him came a friend,
who agreed to act as -a. representative of
the law,; and he promptly told the aston
ished publican that he had orders to leave
a man in possession unless 'the sum owing
to tie Scotch spirit merchant was paid to
him there and then. The publican, of
course, could not pay, so the deserter was
quartered upon him and lived in his house
and at his expense for more than a week
before the deception was discovered. Per
haps the most successful deserterâ for a
time â was William Conrad. Through one
of his comrades he heard of a man whose
son had gone to Australia gold mining,
and had, apparently, disappeared, for
nothing more had been heard of him.
though the parent still believed he would
see him again. Conrad therefore hit upon
the ingenious idea of leaving the army by
stealth, and, going to the man, informing
him that he was. the missing son. As his
knowledge of Australia was considerable,
he lost no time In putting his plant into
execution, and the parent, believing in
him, despite the supposed alteration In
his appearance, provided him with a home
and money galore, a commodity he con
fessed he lacked. But unfortunately for
him, at the end of eighteen months the
real son turned up, and what followed
can better be Imagined than described;

YOUNG MEN'S CHANCES,
On August 15, The Conservative, a week
ly Western review, edited by J. Sterling
Morton of Nebraska City, Nebr., will pub
lish a symposium upon the subject,
"What Are the Young Man's Chances?"
The contributors to this special, en
larged edition of The Conservative are all
representative, . successful and eminent
men ot the West. Among them will be
found such names as W. C. Brown of the
Lake Shore Railroad, E. D. Kenna of the
Santa Fe, the Rev. Jenkln Lloyd Jones,
H. H. Kohlsaat, editor of the Chicago
Record-Herald; President John R. H.
Latchaw of Dcfla'nco College, Robert C.
dowry of . the. Western Union Telegraph
Company, Frank Orren Lowden, M. B. In
galls, president of the. Big Four Railroad,
and many other prominent citizens.
The Inspiration afforded to young men
by such eminent business men should not
be lost. The Conservative Is desirous that
all' of tbe young men in the country
should read these articles.
J. Sterling Morton] has always been
known as a stanch friend to young Ameri
can manhood, end the publication of this
symposium is consistent with his attitude
toward the growing generation for many
years. No ! symposium upon this subject
so comprehensive, broad and complete has
ever, been attempted.' It not only marks
an epoch in this character of literature,
but also evidences tfiat the Conservative
is of invaluable assistance and encourage
ment to the citizenship which is now in
the . course of development.

MAKRiED NAME-O., City. The rea
son that a woman when she marries
changes her name to that of her husband
is that at common law husband and wife
are one and he is that person. That is,
the legal existence of the woman is sus
pended or at least incorporated into that
of the husband under whose protection
she performs everything. When a maid
en marries, she changes her title as well
as her condition. She no longer is Miss
but becomes Mrs., and being one In law
with her husband, she is bound to be
known by his name.
*\l.aus bVR THE NAVYâ F L. B
Petaluma, Cal. All United States battle
ships and first-class cruisers carry a No
1 ensign at the stern, its measurement be
ing 19 feet hoist and 36 feet fly. All other
vessels carry a No. 2 ensign, which meas
ures 14 feet 6 inches hoist and 27 feet 3
inches fly. A No. 1 ensign costs about $43
United f ab Â° Ut **â¢ The flass In the
United States navy are repaired on board
whenever necessary until such time as
they become rotten and worn out. when
they are condemned by survey and burn
ed The total cost of a complete outfit
of flags, both domestic and foreign, with
signal numbers, pennants, repeaters etc
for one ship is about $2400.
DILL PICKLES-G. S., Jamestown, Cal.
The way that dill pickles are prepared i 3
to select small, long cucumbers freshly
gathered, which should not be rubbed A
strong brine of salt and water is poured
over these boiling hot, and allowed to
stand over night. The next day they are
stirred gently to take off the sand, then
drained on a sieve and wiped dry with a
cloth. A pickle of the best white vinegar
ginger, long, round peppers and a little'
garlic is heated and when It comes to a
boil the cucumbers are thrown in and
al owed to boll quickly for three o?.Â£ur
minutea. They are then put Into a jar
with the vinegar and covered closely aft
ward ddlnS a 8Prl * Â° f dilU the Â« e e<Mown-

IMMENSE OREGON LOG RAFT MISSING
SINCE IT LEFT THE COLUMBIA RIVER
Vessels From North Report No Sign of Tugs or Their Tow, Which Went to
Sea Over a Week Agoâ Shipping Men Think They Have Been Driven
Past the Port During the Recent Northwester That Raged on the Coast
AMERICAN SHIP JOHN ENA, NOW LOADING BARLEY AT OAKLAND LONG WHARF FOR NEW YORK. SHE
ARRIVED FROM AUSTRALIA WITH A COAL CARGO LAST MONTH. CAPTAIN MADSEN IS IN COMMAND
OF THE VESSEL. . '
SHIPPING men are wondering what
has become of the big log raft
that left the Columbia River on
the 6th inst. It was in tow of the
Puget Sound tugs Tatoosh and
Richard Holyoke. Since passing out over
the bar nothing has been heard of either
the raft or tugs. None of the coasters
have reported wreckage and the general
supposition is that the tugs and their tow
have been compelled to run past this port
in the recent northwester. One of the
colliers left here for Puget Sound early
in the week and after bucking tho gale
for several days had to put back short of
coal. If the log raft ran into the same
kind of weather the chances are it will
next be heard from coming from the
south.

Deep "Water Ships Arrive.
Among the many arrivals yesterday was
the bark C. D. Bryant from Honolulu.
Bhe was on fire soon after reaching the
islands and comes here for an overhaul
ing.
The cannery tender Thistle arrived from
fgasik and the steamship Edith from

go into the coal trade. Captain Storrs.
late of the Leelanaw, Is in command of
her.
The barkentine Archer, 22 days from
Honolulu, arrived and the ship S. P.
Hitchcock. 52 days from Sydney. K. S. W.,
and the ship Iquique, 56 days from Iqui
que, also made port. Both of the latter
vessels made splendid runs. The Iquique
comes here to load wheat for Europe.
Leading Barley for New York.
The American ship John Ena, command
ed by Captain M. A, Madsen, Is loading
barley at Oakland Long Wharf for New
York. She arrived here from Newcastle.
Australia, on August 26 with 4220 tons or
fcoal and got rid of her cargo several days
ago and is now loading again. The run
from Australia was made in the good
time of 68 days, and had it not been for
heavy weather and head winds the run
would have been made in 50 days. The
Ena will probably sail about the enS of
the month or earlier if the strike is set
tled.
"Water Front Motes.
The transport Warren will sail for Ma
nila next Monday. Among her passen
gers will be about 210 teachers.
Matthew Dunn, a Janitor for Murphy,
Grant & Co., broke his leg on the front
yesterday. The fractured limb was set at
the Harbor Hospital.
The wreck of the steamship Aloha was
sold to Charles E. Naylor, secretary of
the Shipowners' Association, yesterday
for $6150. The chances are that she will
be rebuilt and put in commission again.
The steamship Mariposa will make an
other run to Honolulu before beinp laid
up for an overhauling. The repairs to the
Alameda will not be completed In time to
send her out on the 21st Inst.

NATIVES PERISH
BY THOUSANDS
Dreadful Mortality in
the Indian Tribes
of Alaska.
Â»
Famine Aids the Ravages of
a Disease Resembling
Cholera.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, Sept. 13. â The ravages of the
epidemic of disease among the Indians on
the Lower Yukon last summer was awful.
The mortality reported from Catholic mis
sions Is greater than In any other pre
vious year. Old natives were chiefly the
victims.
Rev. Father Rene has just returned to
Dawson from a trip to Nome and Teller.
He says that thousands of Indians died
lu the area spreading from Nulato down
to St Michael, southward to the Kuskok
wim and northward toward Bering Strait.
Many In the Lower Yukon basin were vic
tims. The epidemic was a sort of cholera
that ran among the natives alone. Father
Rene says It is believed to have been
brought over In some way from the Sibe
rian coast. The shortage of fish and other
food among the natives did a great deal
to spread the plague by rendering the In
dians first - impoverished as to strength
and unable to withstand the disease.
A very strange report is brought by
Father Rene as to the origin of the In
dians, of America. He says that it has
beeen ascertained by the missionaries at
Nulato and those in Arizona and New
Mexico that the Indians of those two wide
ly remote quarters speak the same identi
cal language. The report seems scarcely
credible, but comes from the father direct
as given him by priests under his charge
at Nulato, who have mastered the Nulato
language and who have been correspond
ing with the fathers among the Apaches.
The headquarters of Father Rene are. in
Juneau. He will proceed from Dawson to
Seattle to endeavor to raise fund3 for the
establishment of a Catholic hospital in
Nome. He established a Catholic church
in Nome only a few weeks ago and left a
priest in charge, and soon will have an
assistant sent there.
Minook Swept by Fire.
VICTORIA, B. C, Sept. 13.â The steam
er Cottage City, which arrived here short
ly after midnight, brought news that the
town of Little Minook, on the Lower Yu
kon, has been swept by fire 'and all the
cabins and much valuable property de
stroyed. Forest fires were the cause. -

New York Dried Fruit Market. .
Mail advices from New York say: "Trade is
dull both for spot and future delivery. The de
moralization in raisins, the heavier receipts and
Improvement In quality of fresh fruits now
coming forward and the warmer weather all
have contributed to bring about a more un
satisfactory condition in dried fruits generally.
The trade absolutely refuses to become in
terested In new caliiornla raisins and only a
very small business is doing on spot. Loose
Muscatels are practically neclected. but the
near approach of the Jewish New Year has led
to a little better demand for bleached raisins.
New Valencias are selling in a small way to
arrive early next week at 8c; arrivals ten days
later, 7%c. New crop Sultanas continue very
hiph on the -other tide, due to bad weather.
â¢ "New Santa. Clara prunes are firm on the
coast on a 3Uc basis. Sales of 40-50s. 50-60s
and 60-70s were reported, but the quantity sold
could not be learned. There is virtually noth
ing doing in spot prunes. Apricots and peaches
are dull and unchanged. New nectarines are
offered at 5Â®S%c. f. o. b. coast, with buyers
showing no interest. Old dates are meeting
with a small demand. Stocks here are large
and quotations can be shaded. Fles are well
cleaned up. A small trade is doing in new
figs, due next week, at 12Â®13c for 2 to 2%
inches, 10, 12 and 14 lb boxes.
"Evaporated apples continue their downward
course uninterrupted. Spot prime are offered
freely at S%c without bids and probably Me
less would buy. Future prime is weak at &c,
the only bid heard of being S%c Quotations
on spot chops are entirely nominal. Waste is
quiet at 2S2.15c."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
An area of high pressure is central off the
Washington coast, while tne lowest pressure is
reported,from Southern Arizona. Fair weather
prevails over the Pacific slope except along
Pugfct Sound, where it Is cloudy and threaten
ing. Light showers have occurred in Northern
Washington.
The temperature has risen over California and
the eastern portion of Washington and re
mained nearly stationary in other districts.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, September 14, 190L
Northern Californiaâ Fair Saturday and con
tinued warm; light northwest wind.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Saturday; light
west wind.
Nevadaâ Fair Saturday; fresh northwest
â¢wind.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair Saturday;
continued warm: light northerly wind, becom
ing brlek westerly in the afternoon.
G.' H. WILLSON,
Local Forecast Official, Temporarily in Charge.

New York Stock Market. Â¦
NEW YORK, Sept. 13.â The news of the Pres
ident's relapse fell upon the stock market with
almost greater force than did that of the first
attack upon him. The measure of this effect
Is seen in the result and prices as a rule fell
to-day below the level of last Saturday. The
laborious course of restoration of prices during
the week had carried the averaze well back
to that ruling at the close of last Friday, be
fore the news of the shooting was known in
Wall street. A false report of the President's
death had circulated in London to-day and the
panicky selling from that center was thrown
upon the opening market here. The condition
of the money market and eome of the develop
ments of yesterday left the market vulnerable
even to a more natural course of events Dis
appointment over yesterday's dividend action
on M. Paul was keen and heavy selling of that
stock was inevitable In any event. Amalga
mated Copper also continued under special pres
sure, owing to assertions that enormous stocks
of copper were being accumulated and that thÂ»
maintenance of tlie nrice of the product was
becoming difficult. Republic Steel was actually
affected by the comments on its. poor showing
of earnings for the year. The amount of offer
ings of Government bonds brought out by Sec
retary Gage's proposal was disappointing the
t0 . t i 1 JÂ£ a Â£Â£ ln & only about tf.500.000. compared
with $20,000,000 invited, but payments mad"
for these were in cash unon uresentatlon at
the sub-treasiiry to-day most of the offered
bonds being sold in New York. The apprecia
tion of this situation was seen in the prompt
offerings of bonds at the sub-treasury, the pay-
ments on this account for the day 'reaching
about $ 3, 000, 000. or practically the whole amount
mvcivea in the settlement of the purchase.
ihe critical condition of the money market
during the past week has apparently brought
some relief to the drain on i\ew iTorK 'reserves
and the cash losses are estimated to be no
greater than ?2,5ia),(aK>. This does not include
to-tlay's large gain from the sub-treasury. The
loan item is a matter of mere guess work, but
the tree lending of money eany in the week
to ke*p down the money rate must be reckoned
with, as wen as the liquidation which has been
effected. Call loans went to ltÂ» per cent to
day, but relacsed to 6 per cent. No extraor
dinary measures were deemed necessary by the
clearing-house committee, and in fact that body
was not In session durinsthe day.
Kailroad bonds were weak, but not acutely
as stocks. Total sales. $3,850,000.

London Market.
NE.W YORK, Sept. 13.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The stock market to-day was dominated by
the alarming bulletins regarding the condition
of President McKinley. American stocks
opened from 2 to 3 points under j parity. , The
more- leassurins news caused a slieht im
provement at the end of the flrit hour on the
theory that there was an opportunity to buy
stocks cheap. There was a sharp rally of from
1 to 3 points on the New York opening. Then
followed renewed selling, but . the movement
turned again before the finish, closing about %
above the lowest.

NEW YORK, Sept. 13.â All of the metaMnar
kets were lower to-day.. This is said to be due
to the cablegrams received during the night
on the President's precarious condition. It
also affected local values of tin. ' while- the rest
of the list here remained- at old prices. The
opening found prices off about 25 points. This
level was maintained all day, the market clos
ing unsettled at $25(525 37%. Trade was slow.
At noon values were lower, thus spot stood at
the close at Â£114 11s 6d and futures at Â£112
7s 6d.

in London, spot closing at Â£12.

while the local market closed nominal at $4.

The Supervisors' Judiciary Committee
yesterday recommended the repeal of the
resolution ordering the Board of Public
Works to remove the fences in the Bay
View and University Mound districts. A
number of residents testified that the
fences were a protection, and the matter
will be left to the discretion of the Board
of Works. .-.:,
Fences Will Remain.
THOMAS D. WELLS
DIES OF CANCER
Former Supervisor Suc
cumbs After Long
Illness.
Masons Prepare to Conduct
Funeral at His Old
Home.
PLEASANTON, Sept. 13.^-Ex-Super
\isor Thomas D. Wells died at Ldvermore
this morning of cancer, after a long ill
ness. The malady which caused his death
attacked him about a year ago and In
spite of. the best medical advice and the
loving care of his children he succumbed
to the inevitable this morning and quietly
passed away. '
Thomas D. Wells was born In West Vir
ginia, seventy-three years ago, and came
to California in 1S50 with J. AV. Dougherty.
They went to Jackson, Amador County,
and remained there until 1S68, wlftn Mr.
Wells removed to Dublin, in this valley,
and established a blacksmithing business,
which is now being conducted by his son,
John Wells.
Mr. Wells was Supervisor from this dis
trict for four years. Mrs. Wells died about
ten years ago and the family now con
sists of live grown boys, John, Charles,
Lee, Edward and Thomas Wells, and one
girl, Grace Wells. ,
The funeral will be . held to-morrow
afternoon at Dubin, under the direction
of Mosaic Lodge of Masons, of which the
deceased was a member.
WITTMAlf CASE CONTINUED.
Battery Charge of Prosecution De
layed Pending Decision of Judge.
It was not decided by Judge Seawell yes
terday afternoon whether or not the re
turning of a Jury by the Sheriff in the case
of Captain Wittman was legal. Judge Sea
well l'stened to the arguments of Peter F.
Dunne, who appeared for the police cap
tain, and John S. Barrett on behalf of
Judge Cabanlss for several hours, and
then notified the lawyers* that he would
consider their arguments.
Captain Wittman's case was continued
by Judge Cabanlss until Tuesday, pending
the decision of Judge Seawell.
The case of Captain Wittman was called
In Judge Cabaniss' court in the afternoon.
The jurors summoned by Sheriff Lack
mann were in court. The Judge had been
notiHed that tho argument before Judge
Seawell on the application for a writ of
prohibition was not concluded, and he
continued the caeo'till Tuesday afternoon
at 2 o'clock, the Jurors being notified to
return at that time.
NO PLACES FOB MOLDEES.
Positions Are All Filled at Union
Iron Works.
"We have no places for lronmolders'and
could not supply them even if the strik
ers announced their willingness to return
to work."
This statement was made by
Henry T. Scott, president of the Union
Iron Works, yesterday In a discussion of
the machinists' and moldcrs' strike.
The announcement was made that near
ly 100 men have gone to work this week.
A similar state of affairs was reported at
the Risdon Iron Works, 'although no pre
cise figures were â¢ obtainable. Â¦ Most of
these men are new arrivals from the East
and from points along the Pacific Coast.
The officers of the Iron Trades Council
declare that the situation as far as the
striking machinists is concerned is most
satisfactory. Thrty deny that new men
are being engaged at tho rate claimed by
the employers.
Strike Cases Before Judges.
James Murphy, a striking marine flra
man, appeared before Judge Cabanlss yes
terday on a charge of disturbing tho
peace. He was arrested by Policeman
Herlihy at tho ferry, who alleged that he
was shouting "scab" at non-union men
and attempting to pull them off the cars.
Murphy, who had a decided cockney ac
cent, said that he was only talking to the
men. Tho Judge allowed him to go on
his own recognizance till October 1, but
warned him that if he persisted in his
conduct he would give him tho maximum
Â¦entence.
