Boycott of The Call Tabled.
A resolution declaring a boycott on The
Call was introduced at the regular meet
ing of tho San Francisco Labor Council
held last night. It was presented by
James Bowlan and M. C. Coffey of the
Hackmen's Union. After a discussion of
over an hour the resolution was perma
nently laid on the table. The resolution
stated that the cause of the proposed boy
cott was the stand which The Call h<)d
taken in regard to the existing labor dif
ficulties. . â¢.>.Â¦
No Troops for Port Costa.
SACRAMENTO. Sept. 13.â Sheriff Veale
of Contra Costa County and E. J. Depue
of Port Costa called on Governor Gage to
day to discuss affairs at Port Costa. The
trio were in consultation for more than an

Tehama-Strect Tracks Removed.
The Board of Works yesterday began
the work of removing the tracks on Te
hama street, between Third and Fourth,

ket Street Railway Company. The Super

TEAMSTERS
PLAN REVOLT
FROM RANKS
Union Men Hold Further
Caucuses and Talk
of; Break.
Â¦Â¦ â ?
Effort Among Them to Get a
Hundred Men to Return !
- to "Work.
Brotherhood Quadruples Its Picket
Force in *\frliolesale District
and Close Watch Is Kept
on Members. .
The movement among dissatisfied teams
ters to return to work gathered momen
tum yesterday when, at caucuses on Bush
street', it was decided as soon as 100 union
men had pledged themselves a break
would be made Monday morning.
Some seventy-five men. agreed on Thurs
day to go to work yesterday, but the
prompt interference of Michael Casey,
business agent of the Brotherhood of
Teamsters, together with the appearance
of a quadrupled guard of pickets in the
downtown district, prevented the consum
mation of the plan. The men say-they
are not dismayed, however, and that they
will carry out their intention on Monday,
be the cost to themselves what it may.
Tho only circumstance which prevents
an open break among the teamsters is
the lear of violence" at the hands of the
strikers or sympathizers. This waÂ« the
subject of discussion between Manager
Renner of the Draymen's Association and
a few of the striking teamsters yesterday.
Manager Renner made the following
statement yesterday:
The report has been industriously circulated
among the striking teamsters that the prom
ises made by the Draymen's Association that
waeen and houra will not be changed if - the
men tro back to work will be broken and ths
drivers will bo ground down to the lowest
notch. I wlffh to make the explicit statement
for myself as member of the nrm of Stetson-
Kenner Drayase Company and for all the
members of ,the Draymen's Association that
the wages and hours of all teamsters In the
fuuire, no matter In what way the present
strike is settled, will bo the same as those In
existence at the tlmo the strike was Inaugu
rated, an established by the agreement be
tween the Draymen's Association and the
Hrotherhood of Teamsters October 1, 1900, with
the exception of Sunday and holiday work. On
these daya drivers taking out teams until 12
o'clock noon or any time before that shall get
a full day's pay, and teams kept out all day
time and a half. I wish It to be distinctly
understood that all benefits to drivers will be
the tame whether they are members of unions
or not.
Leading draymen assert that the action
of the brotherhood in increasing- the pick
ets is a positive indication that a break
is feared and that strenuous efforts will
be made to prevent such a culmination of
the strike if possible. The belief is .gen
eral, that the end of the teamsters' strike
is now close at hand and that by Mon
day,. a' decided stampedo will have taken
place.
ATTACK AN ENGLISH RATTER.
British Consul May Appeal for Fed
- eral Protection.
An attack made on a sailor, a Brit'sh
subject, one of the crew of a British ship,
yesterday afternoon will probably be
mdde the basis of an appeal by ,tho Brit
ish Consul to the Federal authorities fur
protection from violence at the hands of
strikers.
Charles Lindsay, an able seaman -be
longing to the British ship Haddon Hall,
was attacked about G o'clock In the after
noon by a mob of union sailors. After
being chased for several blocks he took
refugo In the Seamen's Institute, where
he remained until a policeman arrived
from the harbor station. Sergeant Blank
later escorted aim to his ship. Lindsay
was on his way- up town when attacked.
A number of men approached .him and
ankr-d:
"You belong to the Haddon Hall?"
"Yes, I do."
."And you're working cargo. Take that!"
Ltindxay dodged the blow but in dodging
stopped another one. J'.Iowh mined from
Â«vcry Hide but Iwforo they could pursue*
tho usual tacticsâ knock him down ami
rob himâ he Jumped clear of tho crowd
ntnl took to hla heels with tho mob after
him.
When tho Haddon Hall first came along
side Howard-street wharf tho men called
on tho British Consul for advice au to
whether tho articles they had Â«ignec!
mado it compulsory for them to work the
cargo. They wcro told that their con
tract was to that effect and tfiat any vio
lation of the articles' would be puniqhed
with imprisonment. The men went back
to the ship and have faithfully fulfilled
their contract.
The local Sailors' Union has arrogated
to itself in this ca3e the right to Jay down
the law in regard to protection of labor
ers. When notified of the decision of the
British Consul ths union pickets were
given orders by their officers not to inter
fere with or use violence toward the
crews of British ships, whether they
worked cargo or not. Â¦â¢Â¦
Lindsay, who is colored and a British
subject, declares that the matter has not
ended; that his contract with his em
ployers compeis him to work cargo, and
that as a 'British subject he is entitled to
walk the streets of an American port
without being exposed to murderous at
tack at the hands of American citizens.
As several of the officers of British ships
have been attacked and a number of
others threatened it is more than likely
that the Lindsay case will be brought to
the attention of the Federal authorities
through the British Consul.

Rabbi Nieto Bids, the People
Hope for the Vindica
tion of Truth.
The Hebrew New Tear was ushered In.
at sundown last evening with special ser
vices in the synagogues. According to the
calendar it was the beginning of the first
day of the seventh, month of the Hebrew
sacred year and the beginning of the civil
year 5662. The occasion Is observed as a
sacred festival and all work Is prohibited.
To-day Is set apart for the work of self
examination and preparation for the Day
of Atonement, which comes ten days later.
During the days preceding thÂ© first of
the new year It Is customary to visit the
graves of friends and relatives and give
charity. The distinguishing feature of the
days is the blowing of ram's horn, or Sho
far. The ram's horn In ancient times
seems to have been blown at times of
national danger, or when It became neces
sary to alarm the. inhabitants of a city
or to arouse them, to defense when at
tacked. At the present day It is said the
blowing of the ram's horn signifies the
arousing of man to a knowledge of his de
pendence upon God and his need for pre
paring to defend himself against evil and
to. Justify 'his conduct for the past year
at the tribunal of his own conscience.
After the evening services of last night
and until the eve of the Day of Atone
ment it is customary for friends to greet
each other with, the words, "Mayst thou
be inscribed in the book of life," it being
a part of the Jewish belief that the per
fectly good are Immediately ' Inscribed.
They often add to this greeting the words
"Immediately" to show their appreciation
for the friend's character as a man.
In all of the synagogues services were
held, so as to greet the new year at the
proper season, which is at sunset. '

Talk on Dahlias.
J. W. Bagge, gardener of the Protes
tant Orphan Asylum, appeared before, the
California State Floral Society at Its
monthly meeting yesterday afternoon and
told tho assembled members what he
knew about dahlia cultivation. At the end
of his pleasing address Uagge was given
a vote of thanks. -

Jimmie Whalen proved too much for the
Senators yesterday afternoon at Recrea
tion grounds, and as a. consequence three
scattering hits and one lone mark on the
score board were all that were made off
his puzzling delivery. On the other hand,
the German aggregation fell on Hoffer's
shoots to the extent of three runs and
three times as many safe bumps. The
contest was decidedly a pitchers' battle
up to the seventh round, when the Dutch
began to land on Hoffer. When the shout
ing was all over Hoffer's delivery was
found to have been damaged to the extent
of four clean swr.ts and three tallies. The
Capital City slugg-ers could do absolutely
nothing with "Chimmie der Whale,'.' one
man only making the round trip.
Uncle Hank's men began to do business
in the seventh. Captain Pabst opened
with a hit, went 'to second on Krug's out
and came home on Shay's clean single,
Shay taking second on the throw. By
means of two more hits and a Â¦ pretty
double steal another pair of pretzel shoot,
ers ambled home, which made, the score
board stand 3 to 1, remaining so untllÂ«the
end.
I McLaughlln reached first on Jimmie's
wild throw in the sixth, was moved up to
third on Sheehan's hit and bounded across
the rubber on Courtney's out to Shay and
Pabst. i â¢ '
The Senators played *a perfect fielding
game but lacked the hitting power. The
Wasps, on the other hand, made a few
niisplays. but managed to come in con
tact with the sphere at the right moment.

DUBOSE MAKES
HIS DEFENSE
Denies That He Advised
His , Clients to; Dis
j obey the Writs.
His Statements Are Flatly
Contradicted by Fink
and Knight.
The rehearing granted Dudley Dubose by
the United States Circuit Court of Appeals
was taken up yesterday by United States
Commissioner Heaccck, . E.- S. PiKsbury
appearing a3 amicus curiae and Garret
McEnerney as counsel for Dubose. Du
bCEe was found gulityof c:ntempt of court
several weeks ago and sentenced to six
months' imprisonment in the Alameda
County Jail for having advised his clients
in the celebrated Nome mining litigation
to disobey a writ of siipersedeas issued by
the United States Circuit Court of Ap
peals^in this city. ...
In the rehearing yesterday Attorney
Pillsbury established the fact that con
tempt of court had been committed-, al
though Dubose sought to show that he
had not directly advised disobedience,- but
had made efforts to prevent the writs
from being enforced.
Dubose was the first witness placed on
the stand. He was asked by .McEnerney:
"Did you ever advise or order your cli
ents to disobey any writ issued out of the
United States Circuit Court of Appeals?"
"No. sir.!' was the reply. â¢ ; ,
"You had nothing to 00 with that what
ever?"
"No, sir."
"At the meeting with Major Van Ars
dale did you state that you had advised
your clients to disobey that writ, and
would continue to do so?"
"No. sir."
"Had you any arrangement with any
persoi* whatever to obstruct, interfere
with, disobey -or refuse to comply with
any order issued by this court in any ot
this Nome litigation?"
"No, sir."
Pillsbury declined to cross-examine the
witness. . â¢ â¢
Damaging Testimony Against Duboso
Damaging testimony contradicting that
of Dubose was given by Alfred Fink, a
lawyer doing business in Nome. He nar
rated that the writs of supersedeas
reached Nome September 14, 190u. and on
the afternoon of the next day the attor
neys for both sides appeared before Major
Van Arsdalc. The attorneys for McKenzie
requested him not to assist the defend
ants in enforcing the writs of. superse
deas by helping them to take possession
of a_large quantity of gold dust that was
stored in the bank vaults and was in pos
session of Receiver McKenzie. Messrs.
Metson. Knight and Johnson argued that
the writs were valid, and Dubose. Beam
er and Geary contended that the writs
were void. Receiver McKenzie- had de
clined to obey the writs, and the question
was whether/ or not they were able to be
protected by Major Van Arsdale and the
military in their disobedience.
Dubose stated at that meeting that lie
had advised his clients not to obey the
writs, t and that he would continue to ad
vise ' them so. because he regarded Â¦ the
writs as altogether void.
Knight Corroborates Witness Fink.
Samuel Knight, attorney at law, cor
roborated the testimony of Fink as to the
ndmlssions made. by Dubose on the occa
sion of the conference at Major Van Ars
dale's room. On that occasion Dubose
said: "If you want my opinion, I have ad
vised my clients to disobey the writs."
Dubose being recalled went on to ex
plain what he had said and meant at the
conference spoken of.
"I did not advise McKenzie to disobey
the order of the court." added Dubose,
"because McKerzie had discharged me as
his attorney a month before. I told Fink
that I had advised Chipps that the writs
were void. I never' advised my clients to
disobey the writs. Chipps said to me on
the street. 'Judge, the Swedes have-got us
on the run.' and I said to Ghipps. 'That's
all right; that matter will be argued be
fore the Circuit Court of Appeals in Seat
tle on the 10th of this month.' "
The transcript of the testimony and pro
ceedings will be forwarded to-day to the
United States Circuit Court of Appeals at
Seattle. > .
RAMSAY NAMED
FOR THE PLACE
Successor to Howison on
the Schley Court of
, Inquiry.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 13.â Rear Ad
miral Francis Munroe Ramsay, retired,
was to-day ordered to. duty as a member
of the court of inquiry which is investi
gating the conduct of Rear Admiral
Schley during the war with Spain. Unlike
Rear Admiral Howison, whom he suc
ceeds, Rear Admiral Ramsay ha*s never,
so far as known, publicly â¢ or privately
commented upon Rear Admiral Schley's
behavior and the latter regards him as
entirely acceptable. 'â¢Â¦' :< Â¦
It developed to-day that the Navy De
partment had anticipated that Admiral
Dewey and Rear Admiral Benhaih would
sustain the challenge of Rear Admiral
Howison and two days ago began to con
sider who should be ordered to fill the
vacancy. The decision was finally ma.de
in favor of Rear. Admiral Ramsay, in the
first place because he Is. the choice of
Secretary Long, and, second, because- of
his known fairness and judgment.
Rear Admiral Ramsay, who will succeed
Rear Admiral Â¦ Howison, .was born, in the
District of Columbia on April 5, 1833. He
is in excellent health/mentally and physi
cally. He entered the navy. on October 5,
1S50. He served in various positions, in
accordance with his rank, until 1881, when
as a special mark of tha department's ap
preciation of his services he was appoint
ed superintendent of the Naval Academy.
When he had â¢ completed , his term of
duty at Annapolis he was given command
of the Boston, and upon his relief from
that vessel was appointed Chief of the
Bureau of Navigation, which position he
held until his retirement. He was placed
on the retired list in .1897. In religion,
Admiral Ramsay is a Catholic, and this
fact has given rise to reports that he
would ..be . antagonistic to Schley. â¢Â¦ . The
acceptance of Rear Admiral. Ramsay by
the latter effectually disposes, of - these
reports.- . â¢â¢Â¦ . - ; ." . v :iC '.\.
Because of the death of President Me-
Klnley it is likely that 1 when the court
reconvenes on Monday a further adjourn
ment will at once be taken. Â¦ â¢

COOPER'S FIRST
VICTIM HERE
Bertha Young Will Tes
tify Against the
Swindler.
Intends to Give Her Aid in
the Effort to Convict
â¢4 the Rogue.
. Â« â :
THE luck of "Sir" Harry Westwood
Cooper, alias Ernest Moore Chad
wick and a score - of other
equally high-sounding names, has
changed. "Sir" Harry now stands
but little chance of escaping a long term
in jail.
Miss Bertha Young, the famous swind
ler's second wife, is in town and ready to
take the stand in the interest of the State
and testify that Cooper knowingly com
mitted bigamy when he married the
pretty little Crockett girl, Norine Schnei
der. .This evidence will sweep away the
objection "that was made at the swindler's
former trial, to the effect that his latest
victim could . not testify against him be
cause she was his wife.
Miss Bertha Young was married to
Cooper in June, 1896, at her home in Bris
bane, Queensland, Australia. Miss Young
is the unfortunate young woman whom
the bigamist deserted in Toronto when he
eloped with Ida Maud Campaign. Miss
Campaign was with Â¦ Cooper in this city
when he was convicted and sent to San
yuentin. Miss Young, so far as is known,
was the first victim of the swindling big
amist. She Is a tall, pretty girl, . witn soit
brown eyes and dimpled cheeks. The
story she tells of. how Cooper wooed and
won her is, in many particulars, like the
sad experience of Norine Schneider. When
Cooper met Miss Young he was posing
as a poor physician at Brisbane, little
by little he built round himself a tale in
which he figured as a hero who had fallen
heir to an English estate that grew grad
ually from Â£2000 to Â£18,000. Alter Cooper
married Miss* Young he managed to bor
row Â£400 from her aunt in Melbourne. and
then went with his bride and her little
brother to England and then to Toronto,
where he deserte,d them. !
Miss Young and her mother have been
making their home in Buffalo, N. Y., and
it is owing to the efforts of ex-Chief Lees
that they came out here to testify against
Cooper. , â¢
Cooper's second trial on the charge of
sending a forged telegram to Miss Norine
Schneider will commence before a jury in
Judge Lawlor's court Monday, and Miss
Young' and her 'mother will be called as
witnesses to prove his marriage to her.
It is also likely 'that the Grand Jury will
be asked to bring an indictment against
Cooper on the charge of bigamy.
SHAFFER BUSY
SEEKING PEACE
PITTSBURG, Sept. 13.-The absence of
President Shaffer from Amalgamated
headquarters was noted this morning and
when the day passed and he did not ap
pear the report that he was in New York
was received from several sources. The
officials of the organization declined to
discuss the strike in~any form and said
that they did not know where Shaffer was.
It was learned to-night that President
Shaffer, with several of his advisers, took
the 10 p. m. train for New York, pre
sumably under, an arrangement with the
steel people to settle the strike. It can
not be said positively that this is the case
but all indications point to it.
The indications early in the day were
that a settlement of the strike was looked
for by the Amalgamated men. This be
lief was strengthened by the current issue
of the Amalgamated Journal, which told
of President Shaffer's renewed efforts for
negotiations. His reported trip to New
York is believed to.be the consummation
of these efforts. Until the result is known
no developments are expected in the
strike situation.
JOLIET. 111.. Sept. 13.â Although not
sanctioned by a joint session of the
lodges the steel strike in Joliet is at an
end. An order from President Shaffer is
on the way declaring the strike here off.
The order will not reach here until late
to-night or early to-morrow and the/men
will hold one more joint meeting to-mor
row morning to ratify the action of Presi
dent Shaffer. The mill started at 2 o'clock
this afternoon and many of the strikers
have already returned to work.
Ground to Death. Under Train Wheels
Willie Cook, 13 years of age, son of
Ernest Cook, a" tinsmith living at 7 Aztec
street, was ground to . pieces under\a
Southern Pacific freight train last night
at Valencia and Twenty-fifth streets
Pieces of his body were found scattered
along the track for over a block. An
elder brother recognized the clothing after
the remains had been taken to the
Morgue.
KILLED BY A TRAIN.â A man supposed to
be B. Mollnarl, a Journeyman baker, was
killed last evening in an attempt to board a
train near Baden. From letters found in his
pockets the railroad officials concluded that
he was Molinari. , ; .
SUPREME CUT
AGAINST BOUND
The Supreme Court yesterday decided
that John Farnham and not Patrick Bo
land was legally entitled to the office of
Public Administrator of San Francisco.
The court did not directly order Boland's
immediate removal from office, but de
creed a new trial, with the suggestion that
judgment be given to Farnham.
Farnham's contention, which is sus
tained, is that enough of the ballots cast
for Boland were marked in such a way as
to identify the voters. . Section. 1215 of the.
Political Code says: "No voter shall place
any mark upon his ballot by which it may
be afterwards identified as the one voted
by him." Boland was elected by a ma
jority of 203 votes. Under the law, the Su
preme Court finds that more than this
number must be discarded in the count. .
Farnham objects to five classes of bal
lots, and four of them are declared illegal.
In certain ballots there is found a cross
in squares opposite which there are no
candidates' names. The court holds that
such marks may identify the voter. There
are other ballots where a cross is found
on the lines dividing two squares. Such
ballots, the court holds, should not be
counted. A third class of ballots contains
two crosses after the names of certain
candidates. Identification is again, in this
case, thought to be possible. Again, two
crosses appear in the squares. The court
says that the law orders one cross and
when two appear the vote is illegal.
Farnham also attempts to have votes
cast out where the stubs are not detached,
but the court says that in such cases the
voter is not responsible, but the, persons
who prepared the ballot3. '
The decision of the court was considera
bly accelerated in the case on the ground
that an election is in the nature of a sum
mary proceeding and should be disposed
of with the least possible delay.
Justice Garoutte, Â¦who gives the decis
ion, and with whom Justices Harrison and
Van Dyke concur, instead of ordering Bo
land to retire in favor of Farnham, turns
the case back to the Superior Court again.
His remarks to this effect are as follows: -
In the many recent election contests that
have been presented here by appeal it appears
to have been the rule without exception to re
turn the cause to the trial court for further
hearing when the Judgment has been reversed,
and we will not depart from that practice In
this case./ At the same time, in so dolnsr, the
action of the court must not be construed as
establishing a precedent which will be fol
lowed in every case of this character brought
here on appeal. Â¦ For the foregoing reasons the
Judgment is reversed and the cause remanded..
Boland assumed the duties of the office
of Public Administrator in January. 1900,
and his term would have expired
next January.. Attorney Hassett, his
legal adviser, ' states that the case
will be fought to the end. Â¦
